The Imam's Duty to Fight Rebels and Prophetic Confirmation
Justifies the imam's fight against rebels using the hadith of Ali

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the imam's duty to call rebels to obedience before fighting and how the hadith of Ali provides prophetic confirmation for his actions in Siffin.

Core Idea
An imam must first counsel rebels before fighting; the Prophet confirmed Ali's fight as just.

Sanusi explains the process a legitimate imam must follow when dealing with rebels. Before any conflict, the imam must call them to obedience, explain the error of their ways, and only if they persist may he fight them. This general rule is then applied to the Battle of Siffin, and a hadith of the Prophet to Ali is cited as proof that Ali's fighting over interpretation was as truthful as the Prophet's fighting over revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the progression from general rule to specific application.
2
Identify the steps the imam must take
Find the sequence: first call to obedience, explain error and harm, then fight only if the rebel persists.
This shows the imam's duty is not immediate warfare but patient counsel.
3
See how the hadith supports the application to Siffin
The hadith directly applies the general rule to Ali's fight at Siffin, equating it with the Prophet's fights over revelation.
The hadith is the key proof that the imam's action is correct.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that the hadith justifies any interpretation. Instead, it affirms that Ali's interpretation was the truth because he was the rightful imam.
This guards against misuse of the hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى طاعته، ومن أبى إلا الإصرار على المخالفة كان على الإمام أن يدعوه إلى الطاعة ويبين له خطأ ما هو عليه من الرأي، وما يتولد من ذلك من الضرر بتفريق كلمة الحق وما فيه من شق عصا المسلمين، فإن لم يرجع عن ذلك كان له أن يقاتله حتى يفيء إلى أمر الله، فهو قاتلهم مصيبا في قتالهم مقيمًا ما عليه من حق الله تعالى إذ لم يكن لأحد منازعته في ذلك لثبوت إمامته لما مر من الدلائل، وكذا هذا في قتال أهل صفين. يحققه المروى عن النبي عليه السلام أنه قال له: 'إنك تقاتل على التأويل كما تقاتل على التنزيل' ثم كان قتاله عليه السلام على التنزيل وهو محق فيه، فكذا عليه في قتاله على التأويل يكون المحق في قتاله.
To his obedience. And whoever refuses except persistence in opposition, it is upon the imam to call him to obedience and to show him the error of his opinion, and what results from that of harm by dividing the word of truth and what it contains of splitting the stick of the Muslims. Then if he does not desist from that, it is permissible for him to fight him until he returns to the command of God. So he is the one fighting them, correct in his fighting of them, fulfilling what is upon him of the right of God Almighty, since no one has the right to dispute with him in that due to the establishment of his imamate because of the proofs that have passed. And similarly is this with regard to the fighting of the people of Siffin. This is confirmed by what is narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said to him: 'You fight over interpretation just as you fought over revelation.' Then his fighting, peace be upon him, over revelation, and he was in the truth therein; so likewise, his fighting over interpretation, he is the one in the truth in his fighting.
Source pages p. 1173

Commentary
This passage defines the duty of a legitimate imam towards rebels and provides a prophetic proof for its application to the Battle of Siffin. It begins by stating that if a person refuses obedience, the imam must first call him to obedience and explain the error of his position and the harmful consequences of dividing the Muslims. If that fails, the imam may fight him until he submits to God's command. The imam is correct in fighting such rebels because his imamate is established by prior proofs. The author then applies this general rule specifically to the fighting at Siffin. To confirm this, he cites the hadith where the Prophet told Ali: 'You fight over interpretation just as you fought over revelation.' The Prophet's fighting over revelation was based on truth; therefore, Ali's fighting over interpretation is likewise based on truth. Thus, the imam's action against the rebels at Siffin is justified by both reason and transmitted proof.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'You fight on interpretation as on revelation' means that any interpretation is correct.
Correction: The hadith specifically affirms that Ali's interpretation was the truth because he was the rightful imam, not that all interpretations are valid.

Checkpoint

A) Immediately fight the rebel to restore order.
B) Call the rebel to obedience and explain the error of his opinion.
C) Consult with other scholars and wait for a consensus.

Key Takeaway
A legitimate imam must first counsel rebels before fighting, and the prophetic hadith confirms that such fighting, like that of Ali at Siffin, is just.

Refuting Claims about Talha and Zubayr and Ruling on Rebels
Refutes claims about Talha, Zubayr and defines ruling on rebels

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position on the pledges of Talha and Zubayr and the legal ruling on rebels (bughat) based on the principles of power (man'a) and interpretation (ta'wil).

Core Idea
Rebels with power and interpretation are not liable after submission; thus Talha and Zubayr's stipulation was invalid.

In this passage, the author defends the legitimacy of Ali's caliphate by refuting two accusations against Talha and Zubayr. He also establishes the legal definition of a rebel (baghi) and the conditions under which rebels are held accountable. Understanding these rulings is essential for engaging with historical controversies from a Sunni perspective.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then the literal translation. Note the author's argumentative flow.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two claims being refuted
Find where the author introduces the claims about forced pledge and the stipulation to kill Uthman's killers. How does he refute each?
3
Understand the definition of baghi
The author defines a rebel (baghi) as one having power (man'a) and interpretation (ta'wil). Note how this definition applies to Uthman's killers.
This definition is central to the legal ruling.
4
Examine the ruling on rebel liability
The passage states that when a rebel submits, he is not held liable for past actions. Also note the alternative view and conditions for liability.
Consider why the author emphasizes this ruling.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that there was no liability on Talha and Zubayr, and the imam was justified in not punishing them. How does this connect to the refutations?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعمون أن طلحة والزبير كانا مكرهين على البيعة فاسد لثبوت النقل أن بيعتهما كانت عن طوع على أن خلافته قبل بيعتها كانت ثابتة وما يروى أن طلحة أول من صفعت يده على يد علي فالمراد منه أول يد صفعت يده من أيدي أهل المسجد أو ظن هذا الرأي الراوي أنها أول يد له، لأنه لم يكن حضر البيعة ممن سبق ذكره عند العشاء، وبيعة طلحة صل الله كانت عند الغداة من غد يوم البيعة، وما أنهم قالوا: بايعناك على أن تقتل قتلة عثمان شيء فاسد، فإن قتلة عثمان كانوا روي بغاة، إذ الباغي من له منعة وتأويل، فيقاتل على تأويله الفاسد، ومنعة أولئك ظاهرة، وكانوا في قتله متأولين، إذ كانوا يستحلون ذلك بما نقموا منه من الأمور، والحكم في الباغي أنه إذا انقاد لإمام أهل العدل لا يؤخذ بما سبق منه من إتلاف أموال أهل العدل وسفك دمائهم وجرح أبدانهم، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك أني يستقيم لهم هذا الشرط عليه، وعند بعض الفقهاء أنه كان يؤاخذ بذلك إلا أن الاشتراط على الإمام أن يفعل بأحد الاجتهادين لا محالة فاسد وإذا كان كذلك لم يكن لأحد أن يطلب ذلك من علي ولا كان واجبًا عليهم قتلهم، ولا دفعهم إلى طالب، على أن عند من يرى الباغي مؤاخذا بذلك إنما يوجب على الإمام استيفاء ذلك منهم عند انكسار شوكتهم، وتفرق منعتهم، ووقوع الأمن له عن إثارة الفتنة، وإيقاع الناس في الهرج، وإعضال الداء، وتفاقم الأمر على المسلمين، ولم يكن شيء من هذه المعاني حاصلاً بل كانت الشوكة لهم باقية والقوة ظاهرية بادية والمنعة على حالتها قائمة وعزائم القوم على الخروج على من طالبهم بدمه دائمة، وعند تحقق هذه الأسباب تقتضي السياسة الفاضلة والتدبير الصائب والنظر التام لعامة أهل الإسلام الإعراض عما فعلوا، والإعراض عن مطالبتهم بما استوجبوا، فكيف وليست عليهم تبعة ولا للإمام قبلهم - على أصح القولين وأقوى المذهبين – مطالبة؟!
And what they claim that Talha and al-Zubayr were forced to pledge allegiance is false, due to the established report that their pledge was voluntary, and that his caliphate was established before their pledge. And what is narrated that Talha was the first whose hand was struck on the hand of Ali, the meaning is the first hand struck from among the hands of the people of the mosque, or the narrator held this opinion that it was his first hand, because he had not attended the pledge among those mentioned before at dinner, and Talha's pledge [؟] was at the morning of the day following the day of the pledge. And as for their saying: 'We pledged to you on condition that you kill the killers of Uthman,' that is something false. For the killers of Uthman were regarded as rebels, since the rebel is one who has power and interpretation, so he fights according to his false interpretation, and the power of those was apparent, and they were interpreters in his killing, since they deemed it lawful due to what they objected to from his actions. And the legal ruling concerning the rebel is that if he submits to the imam of justice, he is not held liable for what preceded from him of damage to the property of the people of justice, shedding their blood, and wounding their bodies. And if the matter is so, how can this condition be valid for them? And according to some jurists, he would be held liable for that, but stipulating upon the imam to act according to one of the two ijtihads is necessarily false. And if this is so, no one had the right to demand that from Ali, nor was it obligatory upon them to kill them, nor to hand them over to a claimant. But according to those who hold the rebel liable, that obligates the imam to exact retribution from them only when their strength is broken, their power is dispersed, and security from causing sedition, casting people into chaos, complicating the disease, and aggravating the matter upon the Muslims is attained. And none of these conditions were present; rather, their strength remained, their power was manifest and apparent, their force remained as it was, and the determination of the people to revolt against whoever demanded blood from them was constant. And when these reasons are realized, the excellent policy, correct planning, and complete consideration for the general Muslims dictate turning away from what they did and refraining from demanding what they deserved. So how, when there is no liability upon them, and the imam has no claim against them – according to the soundest of the two sayings and the strongest of the two schools – how can there be a demand?
Source pages p. 1173

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two accusations leveled against Talha and al-Zubayr and a legal analysis of the rebel's liability. First, the claim that they were forced to pledge allegiance to Ali is false because historical reports confirm their pledge was voluntary; Ali's caliphate was valid before their pledge. The report that Talha's hand was the first to strike Ali's hand is misinterpreted – it means his was the first hand among those who pledged in the mosque, not the absolute first. Second, the claim that they pledged on condition that Ali kill the killers of Uthman is false because the killers were rebels. A rebel (baghi) is defined as one who has power (man'a) and an interpretation (ta'wil). The killers had both: they had power and they interpreted Uthman's actions as justifying his killing. The author then states the legal ruling: when a rebel submits to the imam of justice, he is not held liable for any past damage to life, property, or limb. Therefore, the condition is invalid. Some jurists disagree about liability, but even then, demanding retribution is only permissible when the rebels' power is broken and security is assured – none of which existed at the time. Hence, the imam was justified in refraining from punishing them, and there was no liability at all according to the stronger opinion.

الباغي
المنعة
التأويل
الإمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Talha and Zubayr were forced to pledge to Ali and stipulated that Ali must kill Uthman's killers.
Correction: The passage shows their pledge was voluntary and the stipulation is invalid because the killers were rebels with power and interpretation, and rebels are not liable after submission.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the claim that Talha and Zubayr stipulated that Ali must kill Uthman's killers false?

B) Because the killers were rebels with power and interpretation, and rebels are not liable after submission.
C) Because Ali had already killed them before the pledge.
D) Because stipulating conditions in a pledge is forbidden in Islam.

Key Takeaway
The legal ruling on rebels (bughat) protects them from liability for past actions upon submission, and this principle invalidates the condition attributed to Talha and Zubayr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a time when you demanded a specific condition before giving your support to someone. Was that demand truly necessary, or did it stem from a misunderstanding like the one in this lesson? What would it mean for you to submit without conditions, trusting the wisdom of just authority?
?
The author says the excellent policy is to refrain from demanding retribution when it would cause greater chaos. Consider a disagreement in your life—with family, friends, or community—where you insisted on being 'right' or getting justice. Did your insistence heal or harm? Are you willing to let go of your demand for the sake of peace, even if you feel you are owed?
?
The rebels had an interpretation that justified their actions. Have you ever justified a wrong action to yourself because you felt you had a valid reason? Are you willing to submit your own reasoning to the authority of established truth?

Conclusion on Ali's Caliphate and the Ijtihad of Talha and Zubayr
Concludes that Ali's caliphate is valid and Talha and Zubayr were mistaken but excused by ijtihad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author vindicates Ali's caliphate and justifies the actions of Talha and Zubayr through the concept of ijtihad.

Core Idea
Ijtihad can excuse error among qualified scholars, even in grave matters.

This brief passage wraps up a longer discussion on the early Islamic civil strife. The author affirms the legitimacy of Ali's caliphate and explains why Talha and Zubayr, though mistaken, are not blameworthy. Their error stemmed from legitimate ijtihad, a principle that allows for differing opinions among qualified scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the overall meaning. Notice the two separate claims: one about Ali and one about Talha and Zubayr.
The final part of the Arabic is damaged; focus on the clear parts.
2
Identify the two conclusions
The passage first states that Ali's caliphate is correct and he is not to blame for not pursuing Uthman's killers. Then it says Talha and Zubayr erred but are excused due to ijtihad.
These are two distinct points of doctrine.
3
Understand the role of ijtihad
The key excuse for Talha and Zubayr is that they were qualified mujtahids. Their actions, though wrong, were based on their own reasoning, so they are not sinners.
This does not mean their actions were correct, only that they are not held accountable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالوقوف على هذه الجملة ظهر صحة خلافة علي له، واندفاع اللائمة عنه في تركه التعرض لقتلة عثمان فأما أمر طلحة والزبير فقد كان خطأ عندنا غير أنهما فعلا ما فعلا عن اجتهاد، وهما كانا من أهل الاجتهاد إذ [؟] ظاهر الدلائل [؟] يوجب القصاص على [؟]
And by pausing upon this summary, the correctness of the caliphate of Ali appears, and the blame is repelled from him for his leaving off pursuing the killers of Uthman. As for the matter of Talha and al-Zubayr, it was an error according to us, but they did what they did based on ijtihad, and they were among the people of ijtihad, for [؟] apparent proofs [؟] necessitate retaliation upon [؟]
Source pages p. 1174

Commentary
This passage concludes the argument. It states that based on the preceding discussion, the legitimacy of Ali's caliphate is established, and he is free from blame for not punishing the killers of Uthman. Regarding Talha and al-Zubayr, their actions (i.e., opposing Ali in the Battle of the Camel) were wrong, but they are excused because they acted according to their own ijtihad (independent reasoning). Both were qualified to perform ijtihad. The partially preserved text at the end seems to indicate that the apparent proofs necessitated retaliation, but this part is unclear due to OCR damage.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an action is called an error, the person is sinful and doomed.
Correction: In Islamic theology, an error from a qualified mujtahid is excused and not sinful, as long as they made a sincere effort to reach the truth.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Rebel Who Submits: Legal Status and Ali's Justification
Legal argument on the status of a submitting rebel and defense of Ali's actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the legal ruling that a rebel who submits to a just imam is not liable for previous damages, and how this principle, combined with political considerations, vindicates Ali's decision not to punish the killers of Uthman.

Core Idea
Ali's restraint was legally sound and politically wise.

This passage from al-Tabsira addresses a key historical and legal question: was Ali wrong to not punish the killers of Uthman? The author presents a nuanced legal argument based on the category of rebels (baghi) who submit to the just imam, showing that both legal principle and political wisdom supported Ali's decision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before interpreting.
Notice that the author starts with a general legal principle.
2
Identify the general principle
The author states that when a rebel submits to the just imam, he is not held accountable for past offenses. This is the foundation of the argument.
3
Understand the condition for retribution
The author explains that even those who hold rebels accountable only require retribution when the rebels' power is broken and security is achieved. In Ali's time, this condition was not met.
4
See the application to Ali
The author then applies this reasoning to Ali's situation, showing that Ali's decision was justified both legally and politically.
This refutes the common criticism of Ali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الباغي أنه إذا انقاد لإمام أهل العدل لا يؤخذ بما سبق منه من إتلاف أموال أهل العدل وسفك دمائهم وجرح أبدانهم، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك أني يستقيم لهم هذا الشرط عليه، وعند بعض الفقهاء أنه كان يؤاخذ بذلك إلا أن الاشتراط على الإمام أن يفعل بأحد الاجتهادين لا محالة، فاسد وإذا كان كذلك لم يكن لأحد أن يطلب ذلك من [علي الله؟] ولا كان واجبًا عليهم قتلهم، ولا دفعهم إلى طالب، على أن عند من يرى الباغي مؤاخذا بذلك إنما يوجب على الإمام استيفاء ذلك منهم عند انكسار شوكتهم، وتفرق منعتهم، ووقوع الأمن له عن إثارة الفتنة، وإيقاع الناس في الهرج، وإعضال الداء، وتفاقم الأمر على المسلمين، ولم يكن شيء من هذه المعاني حاصلاً بل كانت الشوكة لهم باقية والقوة ظاهرية بادية والمنعة على حالتها قائمة وعزائم القوم على الخروج على من طالبهم بدمه دائمة، وعند تحقق هذه الأسباب تقتضي السياسة الفاضلة والتدبير الصائب والنظر التام لعامة أهل الإسلام الإعراض عما فعلوا، والإعراض عن مطالبتهم بما استوجبوا، فكيف وليست عليهم تبعة ولا للإمام قِبَلَهم - على أصح القولين وأقوى المذهبين – مطالبة؟!. وبالوقوف على هذه الجملة ظهر صحة خلافة علي له، واندفاع اللائمة عنه في تركه التعرض لقتلة عثمان
Regarding the rebel: when he yields to the imam of justice, he is not held accountable for what preceded from him of destroying the property of the people of justice, shedding their blood, and wounding their bodies. And if the matter is so, how can this condition be valid for them? And with some jurists, he is held accountable for that, except that the condition upon the imam to act according to one of the two ijtihads is necessarily invalid. And if it is so, then no one could demand that from [Ali?], nor was it obligatory upon them to kill them, nor to hand them over to a claimant. Moreover, according to those who hold the rebel accountable for that, it only obligates the imam to exact that from them when their might is broken, their support scatters, and security from provoking fitna and casting people into chaos, and the aggravation of the disease, and the exacerbation of the matter for the Muslims is achieved. And none of these meanings were attained; rather their might remained, their power was apparent and manifest, their support stood in its state, and the resolve of the people to rebel against whoever demanded their blood was constant. And when these causes are realized, virtuous policy, sound management, and complete consideration for the generality of Muslims dictate turning away from what they did, and refraining from demanding what they deserved. How so, when there is no liability upon them, nor any claim from the imam upon them — according to the soundest of the two sayings and the strongest of the two schools? And by stopping at this summary, the validity of the caliphate of Ali appeared, and the blame against him for refraining from confronting the killers of Uthman is repelled.
Source pages p. 1174

Commentary
This passage is a legal and theological argument concerning the status of a rebel (baghi) who submits to the just imam, applied to the case of Ali and the killers of Uthman. The author begins by stating the general principle: when a rebel submits to the just imam, he is not held liable for previous damages to property, life, or limb. Then he shows that any condition to the contrary is invalid, because the imam can only act based on ijtihad. Consequently, no one could demand retribution from Ali (the just imam) for not punishing the killers of Uthman. The author then clarifies that even according to those who do hold the rebel accountable, this accountability only applies when certain conditions are met: the rebels' power must be broken and security restored. Since in Ali's time the rebels still had strength and resolve, the imam was not obligated to exact retribution. Rather, political wisdom called for turning away. Moreover, on the soundest opinion, there was no liability at all. Thus Ali's caliphate is vindicated and blame against him for not pursuing Uthman's killers is refuted.

Key Terms
الباغي
إمام أهل العدل
السياسة الفاضلة
اجتهاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ali was wrong not to punish the killers of Uthman immediately.
Correction: The author shows that Ali's decision was legally justified: rebels who submit are not liable, and even if they were, the conditions for retribution were not met. Political wisdom also dictated restraint.

Checkpoint
According to the author, under what condition must the imam exact retribution from rebels who submit?
A) Immediately upon their submission, regardless of circumstances.
B) When the rebels' power is broken and security from fitna is achieved.
C) Only if the rebels had committed major sins like murder.
D) Never, because rebels are never liable for damages.

Key Takeaway
Ali's decision not to immediately punish Uthman's killers was legally sound and politically prudent given the circumstances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you insisted on immediate justice for someone's wrongdoing, only to realize later that it caused greater division or harm? Are you willing to trust God's wisdom and delay retribution for the sake of long-term peace, even if it means being misunderstood by others?
?
Think of a time you were blamed for not acting quickly against an injustice. Did you have the courage to explain the bigger picture, or did you cave to pressure? What would it cost you to follow Ali's example of patient prudence, knowing that your reputation might suffer?
?
Do you truly believe that God's justice is perfect and will prevail, even when you cannot see it immediately? How does your desire for control and immediate results reveal a lack of trust in His timing and wisdom?

Talha, Zubayr, and Aisha: Ijtihad and Repentance
The author explains that Talha and Zubayr acted through ijtihad and later repented.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi school considers Talha, al-Zubayr, and Aisha excused for their actions in the Battle of the Camel, based on ijtihad and subsequent repentance.

Core Idea
Ijtihad and repentance excuse even serious errors among the Companions.

This lesson addresses a sensitive historical event from a Maturidi theological perspective. The author explains that the actions of Talha, al-Zubayr, and Aisha, though mistaken, were based on valid ijtihad and were followed by sincere repentance. Thus, they remain among the righteous Companions, free from sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the author's tone and key terms like ijtihad and ta'wil.
Notice the author begins with an admission of error before justifying it.
2
Identify the justification for their actions
The author says the apparent evidence demanded retaliation against Uthman's killers. Understand how this led Talha and Zubayr to oppose Ali.
The phrase 'ظاهر الدلائل يوجب القصاص' is crucial.
3
Understand the concept of ta'wil
The author introduces ta'wil (interpretation) as a hidden knowledge that annuls accountability. Recognize that their actions remained within ijtihad despite being erroneous.
Ta'wil here means a valid but incorrect interpretation.
4
Reflect on their repentance
Read the reports of Aisha's weeping and Talha's final pledge to Ali. These demonstrate sincere repentance, which removes sin.
Repentance is a key theme: they withdrew and regretted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما أمر طلحة والزبير فقد كان خطأ عندنا غير أنهما فعلا ما فعلا عن اجتهاد، وهما كانا من أهل الاجتهاد إذ ظاهر الدلائل يوجب القصاص على قاتل العمد واستئصال شأفة من قصد سلطان الله تعالى بالتوهين ودم إمام المسلمين بالإراقة، فبينا الأمر على هذا الظاهر، فأما الوقوف على إلحاق التأويل وإن كان يعد فاسدًا فالصحيح في حقه إبطال المؤاخذة بما يؤشر به، فهو علم خفي قاربه [علي الله؟] وحرماه، ولكن لم يخرج فعلهما بذلك عن حد الاجتهاد فكانا مجتهدين أخطأ في اجتهادهما ثم لاح لهما الأمر بعد ذلك، فانحازا عن المركز، وندم الزبير على ذلك، وكذا طلحة، وكذا عائشة - رضي الله عنهما ندمت على ذلك، وكانت تبكي حتى تبل خمارها وكانت تقول: وددت لو كان لي عشرون ولدًا من رسول الله كلهم مثل عبد الرحمن بن عتاب بن أسيد وأنني ثكلتهم، ولم يكن مني ما كان يوم الجمل - وروى طلحة قال لشاب من عسكر علي وهو يجود بنفسه: امدد يدك أبايعك لأمير المؤمنين - أراد - والله أعلم -: أن يموت وهو في بيعة إمام عادل.
As for the matter of Talha and al-Zubayr: it was an error according to us, but they did what they did based on ijtihad, and they were among the people of ijtihad, for the apparent indications require retaliation against the intentional killer and uprooting the root of one who intends to weaken the authority of Allah the Exalted and spill the blood of the Imam of the Muslims. So we were upon this apparent state. As for stopping at applying ta'wil, even if it is considered invalid, the correct concerning him is the annulment of accountability by what it indicates, for it is a hidden knowledge that [Ali?] and its sanctity approached, but their action did not thereby exit the limit of ijtihad, so they were mujtahidun who erred in their ijtihad. Then the matter became clear to them after that, so they withdrew from the center (of battle). al-Zubayr regretted that, and likewise Talha, and likewise Aisha - may Allah be pleased with them - regretted that, and she used to weep until she would wet her veil and say: 'I wish I had twenty sons from the Messenger of Allah, all like Abd al-Rahman ibn Attab ibn Usayd, and that I had lost them, and that what happened on the Day of the Camel had not happened to me.' And it is reported that Talha said to a youth from Ali's army while he was giving up his soul: 'Extend your hand so that I may pledge allegiance to you to the Commander of the Faithful' - he intended, and Allah knows best - to die while in the pledge of a just imam.
Source pages p. 1174

Commentary
This passage is a historical-theological explanation of the actions of Talha, al-Zubayr, and Aisha (may Allah be pleased with them) during the Battle of the Camel. The author begins by stating that their actions were erroneous (khatā') according to the Maturidi school, but they acted based on ijtihad. He justifies this by noting that the apparent evidence pointed to the necessity of retaliation against the killers of Uthman, so they thought it was their duty. However, the truth was more subtle: the application of ta'wil (interpretation) annuls accountability. Their actions remained within the scope of ijtihad; they were mujtahidun who erred. Later, the truth became clear to them, so they withdrew from the conflict and repented. The author supports this with reports of Aisha's intense regret and weeping, and Talha's dying act of pledging allegiance to Ali, showing he intended to die under a just imam. Thus, despite the error, they are excused by ijtihad and repentance.

Key Terms
اجتهاد
تأويل
ندم
قاتل العمد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Talha, Zubayr, and Aisha were guilty of major sin or disbelief for opposing Ali.
Correction: The author shows they were excused because they acted based on ijtihad and later repented sincerely.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why did Talha and Zubayr initially oppose Ali?
A) Out of worldly ambition.
B) Because the apparent evidence required retaliation against Uthman's killers.
C) Because they did not recognize Ali's caliphate.
D) Because they were forced by others.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi school holds that actions based on sincere ijtihad, even if erroneous, are excused, and repentance restores the actor's standing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Recall a time you were absolutely certain you were right, only to later discover you were wrong. Did you have the humility to withdraw and repent as Talha and al-Zubayr did, or did pride keep you in error? What would it cost you today to apologize to someone you have wronged?
?
Aisha wept until she wet her veil, wishing she had lost twenty sons instead of opposing Ali. Is there a sin or mistake in your past that you have truly wept over and sought forgiveness for? If not, are you carrying a burden of un-repented error? What step will you take this week to 'withdraw' from that mistake and seek Allah's pardon?
?
The author says that even invalid ta'wil can annul accountability if it is done sincerely. Do you extend that same grace to others who act out of misunderstanding, or do you hold their errors against them permanently? Write down the name of one person you need to forgive today, and commit to releasing that grudge as Allah forgives sincere ijtihad.

The Companions' Ijtihad and Regret
Al-Nasafi explains that Companions were mujtahids who erred and regretted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Companions who participated in the Battle of the Camel acted as mujtahids, and despite their error, they are excused because they later regretted their actions.

Core Idea
The Companions were mujtahids who erred but repented, so they are not blameworthy.

This passage from Al-Nasafi addresses a sensitive historical event: the Battle of the Camel. Instead of condemning the Companions involved, he explains their actions as ijtihad (independent reasoning) that turned out to be mistaken. The passage emphasizes that once the truth became clear, they sincerely regretted their mistakes, demonstrating their piety and integrity. Understanding this helps us maintain proper respect for the Companions while acknowledging historical disagreements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the argument. Notice the names mentioned and the narrative of regret.
The OCR has a minor uncertainty in the middle, but the overall meaning is clear.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the statement that directly says the Companions were 'mujtahids who erred in their ijtihad.' Understand that this classifies their action as a sincere but mistaken effort.
3
Look for evidence of regret
The passage provides specific examples: Aisha's weeping and wish, Talha's deathbed allegiance. Notice how these show they recognized their mistake and repented.
This reinforces the idea that they were not sinful but sincere.
4
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Consider why this distinction matters. It protects the status of the Companions while allowing honest historical discussion. It also illustrates the concept of error in ijtihad.
The common misunderstanding is that these Companions are blameworthy; the passage corrects that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما الوقوف على إلحاق التأويل وإن كان يعد فاسدًا فالصحيح في حقه إبطال المؤاخذة بما يؤشر به، فهو علم خفي قاربه علي الله [؟] وحُرماه، ولكن لم يخرج فعلهما بذلك عن حد الاجتهاد فكانا مجتهدين أخطأ في اجتهادهما ثم لاح لهما الأمر بعد ذلك، فانحازا عن المركز، وندم الزبير على ذلك، وكذا طلحة، وكذا عائشة رضي الله عنهما ندمت على ذلك، وكانت تبكي حتى تبل خمارها وكانت تقول: وددت لو كان لي عشرون ولدًا من رسول الله كلهم مثل عبد الرحمن بن عتاب بن أسيد وأنني ثكلتهم، ولم يكن مني ما كان يوم الجمل وروى طلحة قال لشاب من عسكر علي وهو يجود بنفسه: امدد يدك أبايعك لأمير المؤمنين أراد والله أعلم أن يموت وهو في بيعة إمام عادل.
As for stopping at the attribution of interpretation (ta'wil) even if it is considered invalid, the correct [view] regarding him is to nullify the blame for what is indicated by it. It is a hidden knowledge that Ali [and?] and his [two?] approached, but their action did not thereby depart from the scope of ijtihad. So they were mujtahids who erred in their ijtihad. Then the matter became clear to them afterwards, so they withdrew from the position. Zubayr regretted that, and likewise Talha, and likewise Aisha (may Allah be pleased with them) regretted that. She used to weep until she wet her headscarf and would say: 'I wish I had twenty children from the Messenger of Allah, all like Abd al-Rahman ibn Attab ibn Usayd, and that I had lost them, and that what happened on the Day of the Camel had not happened to me.' And it is narrated that Talha said to a young man from Ali's army while he was dying: 'Extend your hand so that I may pledge allegiance to you for the Commander of the Faithful.' He intended – and Allah knows best – to die while in the allegiance of a just Imam.
Source pages p. 1175

Commentary
This passage explains that the actions of Ali, Talha, Zubayr, and Aisha during the Battle of the Camel were based on ijtihad (independent reasoning). Although some consider their interpretation (ta'wil) invalid, the correct view is that they are not to be blamed because their actions were based on a hidden knowledge. They were mujtahids who made a mistake (khati'), but later the truth became clear to them and they regretted. It then narrates Aisha's deep regret and Talha's deathbed repentance by pledging allegiance to Ali.

Key Terms
تأويل
اجتهاد
مجتهد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Companions who fought Ali in the Battle of the Camel were sinful rebels.
Correction: The passage explains they were mujtahids who erred and later regretted; thus they are not blameworthy.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct view regarding the participants in the Battle of the Camel?
A) They are all sinful.
B) They were mujtahids who erred and regretted.
C) They were all correct.
D) They are not to be discussed.

Key Takeaway
The Companions involved in the Battle of the Camel were mujtahids who made sincere errors, repented, and are not to be blamed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Talha's final act was to pledge allegiance to the one he had opposed. Is there someone you have mistreated or misjudged that you need to reconcile with, even if it takes humility on your part?

The Maturidi View on Ijtihad and Error in the Battle of the Camel
Al-Nasafi contrasts two theological views on the Battle of the Camel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position on the correctness of the Companions in the Battle of the Camel and how it differs from the view that all were correct.

Core Idea
Only Ali was correct, but the others' error was not sinful.

This passage from Al-Nasafi compares two theological positions on the Battle of the Camel. While some scholars held that all involved were correct due to ijtihad, the Maturidi view affirms Ali's correctness while excusing the others from sin. Understanding this distinction is crucial for a balanced view of early Islamic conflicts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the basic comparison.
Notice the contrasting phrases: 'كلهم مصيبون' versus 'علي هو المصيب'.
2
Identify the two views
The passage presents two positions: the view that all were correct, and the Maturidi view that only Ali was correct.
The first view is attributed to some hadith theologians; the second is 'عندنا' (with us).
3
Understand the Maturidi nuance
The Maturidis affirm Ali's correctness but do not declare the others sinful. Their error did not reach the level of fisq (transgression).
This nuance distinguishes Maturidi from both extreme views.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider how this position balances respect for all Companions while affirming a correct ruling. This approach avoids both declaring all equally correct and condemning the others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن بعض متكلمي أهل الحديث كان يقول: كل ما كان منهم كان مبنيًا على الاجتهاد وكل مصيب، فكان على رأي هذا كلهم مصيبون إذ كان من مذهبه أن كل مجتهد في فروع الدين مصيب، وعندنا وإن لم يكن كذلك، وكان علي هو المصيب دون غيرهم إلا أنهم لم يبلغوا في خطئهم مبلغ الفسق.
While some theologians of the hadith folk used to say: Everything that was from them was based on ijtihad and everyone was correct (musib). So according to this view, all of them were correct, because it was his madhhab that every mujtahid in subsidiary matters of religion is correct. But with us, it is not so. Rather, Ali was the correct one, not others, except that they did not reach the level of transgression (fisq) in their error.
Source pages p. 1175

Commentary
This passage contrasts two positions. The first, held by some hadith theologians, is that all participants in the Battle of the Camel were correct (musib) in their ijtihad, meaning that in matters of ijtihad (subsidiary matters), every mujtahid is correct. The second is the Maturidi position: Ali was the one who attained the correct ruling; the others erred. However, their error did not amount to sin (fisq) because it was based on valid ijtihad. The passage emphasizes that while they were wrong, they are not declared sinners.

Key Terms
مصيب
فسق
فروع الدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi view holds that all Companions in the Battle of the Camel were equally correct.
Correction: The Maturidi view actually holds that only Ali was correct, but the others' error was not sinful.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi view, what is the status of the Companions who opposed Ali in the Battle of the Camel?
A) They were sinful (fasiq).
B) They were correct (musib).
C) They erred but not to the level of sin.
D) They were disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi position affirms Ali's correctness while recognizing that the others' ijtihad-based error did not make them sinners.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are certain you are right in a disagreement, do you ever question whether your own ijtihad could be flawed? How does the Maturidi acknowledgment that even sincere ijtihad can miss the correct ruling challenge your confidence in your own judgments today?
?
If Ali alone was correct in that conflict, do you feel the weight of how difficult it is to discern truth when both sides are sincerely striving? Are you willing to extend the same benefit of the doubt to those who oppose you in matters where ijtihad applies?
?
The passage teaches that error based on ijtihad does not make one a sinner. Is there someone you have considered sinful simply because they reached a different conclusion on a matter that is open to ijtihad? Can you commit to reassessing that judgment in light of this lesson?

Aisha's Intent: Reconciliation, Not War
Al-Nasafi transmits a report clearing Aisha of intent to fight Ali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Aisha did not intend to fight Ali but sought reconciliation, and that Ali honored her after the battle.

Core Idea
The first narration clears Aisha of aggressive intent in the Battle of the Camel.

Al-Nasafi begins by transmitting a report from Aisha herself that she had no intention of fighting Ali. She sought only to make peace between the two groups, but war broke out unintentionally. Afterward, Ali treated her with great honor and escorted her back to Medina. This lesson examines that report and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the first narration
Read the Arabic and translation of the first report, focusing on Aisha's stated intention and Ali's treatment of her.
Note that the report emphasizes Aisha's desire for reconciliation, not war.
2
Identify the key claims
Identify three claims: (1) Aisha did not fight Ali, (2) she intended reconciliation, (3) Ali honored her afterward.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this report is important for understanding the Companions' relationships and the nature of the battle.
This report counters narratives of personal enmity between Aisha and Ali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد روی أن عائشة رضي الله عنها وعن أبيها لم تحارب عليا ولا حاربها علي، وإنما قصدت عائشة الإصلاح بين الطائفتين فوقع الحرب بينهما، ثم أكرم علي عائشة وردها إلى المدينة مكرمة مصونة.

وروى أبو بكر الباقلاني أحد متكلمي أهل الحديث عن بعض الأجلة من أهل العلم: أن الواقعة بينهم كانت على غير عزيمة على الحرب بل كانت فجأةً وعلى سبيل دفع كل واحد من الفريقين عن أنفسهم، لظنه أن الفريق الآخر قد غدر به لأن الأمر كان قد انتظم بينهم وتم الصلح والتفرق على الرضا، فخاف قتلة عثمان من التمكن منهم والإحاطة بهم، فاجتمعوا وتشاوروا واختلفوا ثم اتفقت آراؤهم على أن يصيروا فريقين ويبدأوا بالحرب في المعسكرين، ويختلطوا ويصيح الفريق الذي في عسكر علي له به غدر طلحة والزبير، ويصيح الفريق الثاني: غدر علي، فتم لهم ذلك، ونشبت الحرب، فكان كل فريق منهم دافعا عن نفسه، وهذا صواب من الفريقين، قال أبو بكر الباقلاني: هذا هو الصحيح، وعلى هذا الرأي اندفعت اللائمة عن الفريقين، ثم كيفما دارت القصة فنحن نعلم أن عليًا وطلحة والزبير كانوا من العشرة الذين بشروا بالجنة، وكذا عائشة كانت على ما كانت عليه من الدرجة.
And it has been narrated that Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her and her father) did not fight Ali, nor did Ali fight her. Rather, Aisha intended reconciliation between the two parties, but war occurred between them. Then Ali honored Aisha and sent her back to Medina, honored and protected.

And Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, one of the theologians of the hadith folk, narrated from some eminent scholars: that the incident between them was not with determination on war, but happened suddenly as a means of each party defending themselves, because each thought that the other party had betrayed them. For the matter had been settled between them, peace was concluded, and they had dispersed in satisfaction. Then the murderers of Uthman feared being captured and surrounded, so they gathered, consulted, differed, and then agreed to form two groups and start the war in both camps, and mix in. The group in Ali's camp would shout: 'Talha and Zubayr have betrayed,' and the second group would shout: 'Ali has betrayed.' And they succeeded in that, and the war broke out. So each party was defending themselves. And this is correct from both parties. Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said: This is the correct view. And with this view, blame is removed from both parties. Then however the story goes, we know that Ali, Talha, and Zubayr were among the ten who were given glad tidings of Paradise, and likewise Aisha was of the level she was.
Source pages p. 1175

Commentary
This passage provides two narrations that absolve the Companions of intentional fighting. The first narration (from Aisha herself) states that she did not intend to fight Ali, but only sought reconciliation, and that Ali treated her honorably. The second narration, from al-Baqillani, presents a detailed account: a truce had been reached, but the murderers of Uthman incited the war by starting the fighting in both camps, making each side believe the other had betrayed. Thus, the Companions acted in self-defense, not aggression. Baqillani considers this the correct explanation, which removes blame from both parties. The passage concludes by affirming the high status of these Companions as among the ten promised Paradise.

Key Terms
إصلاح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Aisha intentionally fought Ali out of personal hatred.
Correction: This report shows she intended reconciliation, not war.

Checkpoint
According to the first narration, what was Aisha's intention in the Battle of the Camel?
A) To fight Ali.
B) To make peace between the two parties.
C) To support the murderers of Uthman.
D) To take control of the caliphate.

Key Takeaway
Aisha did not intend to fight Ali; she sought reconciliation, and war occurred unintentionally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you enter a disagreement, do you truly seek reconciliation, or is there a part of you that wants to win? How can you emulate Aisha's pure intention for peace, even if the outcome is out of your control?
?
Think of a time your good intentions led to an unintended conflict. How did you treat the other person after? Are you willing to honor them and let go of pride, as Ali did with Aisha?
?
What would it take for you to put aside your own narrative and genuinely seek reform (إصلاح) in a relationship you value? Will you take one concrete step today to move toward peace, even if you did not start the conflict?

Al-Baqillani's Account: A Conspiracy by Uthman's Murderers
Al-Nasafi transmits al-Baqillani’s account of a conspiracy by Uthman’s murderers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Baqillani's explanation that the Battle of the Camel was instigated by the murderers of Uthman, not by the Companions' design, and that each party acted in self-defense.

Core Idea
The battle was a conspiracy by Uthman's murderers, not a result of Companion disputes.

Al-Nasafi then transmits a detailed account from Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, which reveals that the battle was not planned by the Companions. A peace had been reached, but the murderers of Uthman feared retribution and orchestrated a false flag attack to make each side think the other had betrayed. Consequently, the Companions fought in self-defense. Al-Baqillani declares this the correct view that removes blame from all parties. This lesson examines that account and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the second narration
Read the Arabic and translation of al-Baqillani's account carefully, noting the sequence of events.
Pay attention to the role of the murderers of Uthman.
2
Trace the conspiracy plan
Identify the steps: (1) peace concluded, (2) murderers fear capture, (3) they conspire, (4) they divide into two groups, (5) they start shouting betrayal in each camp, (6) war breaks out.
This shows the battle was deliberately provoked by outsiders.
3
Understand the conclusion
Note al-Baqillani's verdict: this explanation removes blame from both parties, and all Companions involved are among the ten promised Paradise.
This preserves the honor of the Companions despite the conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد روی أن عائشة رضي الله عنها وعن أبيها لم تحارب عليا ولا حاربها علي، وإنما قصدت عائشة الإصلاح بين الطائفتين فوقع الحرب بينهما، ثم أكرم علي عائشة وردها إلى المدينة مكرمة مصونة.

وروى أبو بكر الباقلاني أحد متكلمي أهل الحديث عن بعض الأجلة من أهل العلم: أن الواقعة بينهم كانت على غير عزيمة على الحرب بل كانت فجأةً وعلى سبيل دفع كل واحد من الفريقين عن أنفسهم، لظنه أن الفريق الآخر قد غدر به لأن الأمر كان قد انتظم بينهم وتم الصلح والتفرق على الرضا، فخاف قتلة عثمان من التمكن منهم والإحاطة بهم، فاجتمعوا وتشاوروا واختلفوا ثم اتفقت آراؤهم على أن يصيروا فريقين ويبدأوا بالحرب في المعسكرين، ويختلطوا ويصيح الفريق الذي في عسكر علي له به غدر طلحة والزبير، ويصيح الفريق الثاني: غدر علي، فتم لهم ذلك، ونشبت الحرب، فكان كل فريق منهم دافعا عن نفسه، وهذا صواب من الفريقين، قال أبو بكر الباقلاني: هذا هو الصحيح، وعلى هذا الرأي اندفعت اللائمة عن الفريقين، ثم كيفما دارت القصة فنحن نعلم أن عليًا وطلحة والزبير كانوا من العشرة الذين بشروا بالجنة، وكذا عائشة كانت على ما كانت عليه من الدرجة.
And it has been narrated that Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her and her father) did not fight Ali, nor did Ali fight her. Rather, Aisha intended reconciliation between the two parties, but war occurred between them. Then Ali honored Aisha and sent her back to Medina, honored and protected.

And Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, one of the theologians of the hadith folk, narrated from some eminent scholars: that the incident between them was not with determination on war, but happened suddenly as a means of each party defending themselves, because each thought that the other party had betrayed them. For the matter had been settled between them, peace was concluded, and they had dispersed in satisfaction. Then the murderers of Uthman feared being captured and surrounded, so they gathered, consulted, differed, and then agreed to form two groups and start the war in both camps, and mix in. The group in Ali's camp would shout: 'Talha and Zubayr have betrayed,' and the second group would shout: 'Ali has betrayed.' And they succeeded in that, and the war broke out. So each party was defending themselves. And this is correct from both parties. Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said: This is the correct view. And with this view, blame is removed from both parties. Then however the story goes, we know that Ali, Talha, and Zubayr were among the ten who were given glad tidings of Paradise, and likewise Aisha was of the level she was.
Source pages p. 1175

Commentary
This passage provides two narrations that absolve the Companions of intentional fighting. The first narration (from Aisha herself) states that she did not intend to fight Ali, but only sought reconciliation, and that Ali treated her honorably. The second narration, from al-Baqillani, presents a detailed account: a truce had been reached, but the murderers of Uthman incited the war by starting the fighting in both camps, making each side believe the other had betrayed. Thus, the Companions acted in self-defense, not aggression. Baqillani considers this the correct explanation, which removes blame from both parties. The passage concludes by affirming the high status of these Companions as among the ten promised Paradise.

Key Terms
فجأة
دفع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Battle of the Camel was a deliberate war between Companions over power.
Correction: Al-Baqillani shows it was a misunderstanding caused by conspirators, with each side acting in self-defense.

Checkpoint
According to al-Baqillani's account, who directly instigated the fighting in the Battle of the Camel?
A) Aisha and Talha.
B) Ali.
C) The murderers of Uthman.
D) The Byzantine spies.

Key Takeaway
Al-Baqillani's account attributes the battle to a conspiracy by Uthman's murderers, removing blame from the Companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about conflicts among Muslims, do you tend to assume the worst about those involved? How does this account of the Battle of the Camel—a conspiracy from external forces—challenge you to reconsider your judgments and instead seek explanations that preserve the honor of fellow believers?
?
Is there a conflict in your own life where you have blamed someone without considering external factors or misunderstandings? How can the lesson that 'each party was defending themselves' move you to investigate more deeply before assigning blame?
?
If you were in Ali's or Talha's position, caught in a battle you did not intend, would you have the iman to trust that your brothers acted correctly? What can you do now to cultivate that same trust in your fellow Muslims, especially when you face disagreements?

The Battle of the Camel: A Historical Clarification
Opens with excusable ignorance, then narrates the Battle of the Camel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the accidental nature of the Battle of the Camel and the excusable ignorance of the participants as narrated by Abu Bakr al-Baqillani.

Core Idea
The Battle of the Camel was a tragic accident, not a deliberate sin.

This lesson explores the first part of the source, where the author discusses a type of ignorance that is excusable when combined with seeking knowledge. It then presents the historical account of the Battle of the Camel, emphasizing that Aisha did not intend war and that the battle was sparked by the killers of Uthman. Abu Bakr al-Baqillani's narration provides a key perspective: both groups were defending themselves, thus both were correct.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening on ignorance
Notice how the author describes a kind of ignorance that is accompanied by seeking knowledge and caution, making it excusable. This sets the stage for excusing the Companions.
The phrase 'هذا النوع من الجهل عند نفسه علمه' highlights the subjective aspect.
2
Examine the historical narrative
Read the report that Aisha sought reconciliation, not war. Understand the context: the killers of Uthman provoked the battle.
The author uses the narration to absolve Aisha and Ali of intentional fighting.
3
Analyze Baqillani's account
Focus on the claim that both groups were defending themselves due to a misunderstanding. This is the key doctrinal point: no one is blameworthy.
Baqillani's statement 'وهذا صواب من الفريقين' is crucial.
4
Reflect on the outcome
Consider how this account upholds the integrity of all parties involved, aligning with the Sunni principle of respecting the Companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا النوع من الجهل عند نفسه علمه طلب الكشف والاستعانة بمن يثق به والاجتهاد في التيقظ والتحفظ، وليس مع التأويل في علمه ورأيه أنه قد بقي عليه حق لم يَفِ به أو كان منه ما منعه عن حصول المقصود، فصار هذا لذلك أبلغ في العذر له وأعظم في معنى الجهالة لما معه بعض معاني الناسي عما يكون عليه من الحق في غيره، والله أعلم.
وقد روي أن عائشة - رضي الله عنها وعن أبيها - لم تحارب عليا ولا حاربها علي، وإنما قصدت عائشة الإصلاح بين الطائفيتن فوقع الحرب بينهما، ثم أكرم علي عائشة وردها إلى المدينة مكرمة مصونة.
وروى أبو بكر الباقلاني أحد متكلمي أهل الحديث عن بعض الأجلة من أهل العلم: أن الواقعة بينهم كانت على غير عزيمة على الحرب بل كانت فجأةً وعلى سبيل دفع كل واحد من الفريقين عن أنفسهم، لظنه أن الفريق الآخر قد غدر به لأن الأمر كان قد انتظم بينهم وتم الصلح والتفرق على الرضا، فخاف قتلة عثمان من التمكن منهم والإحاطة بهم، فاجتمعوا وتشاوروا واختلفوا ثم اتفقت آراؤهم على أن يصيروا فريقين ويبدأوا بالحرب في المعسكرين، ويختلطوا ويصيح الفريق الذي في عسكر علي له به غدر طلحة والزبير، ويصيح الفريق الثاني: غدر علي، فتم لهم ذلك، ونشبت الحرب، فكان كل فريق منهم دافعا عن نفسه، وهذا صواب من الفريقين، قال أبو بكر الباقلاني : هذا هو الصحيح، وعلى هذا الرأي اندفعت اللائمة عن الفريقين، ثم كيفما دارت القصة فنحن نعلم أن عليًا وطلحة والزبير كانوا من العشرة الذين بشروا بالجنة، وكذا عائشة كانت على ما كانت عليه من الدرجة الرفيعة، فمن بسط لسانه فيهم بالطعن فهو المطعون في دينه المحكوم عليه بالضلال والبدعة - عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك، وبالإحاطة بهذه الجملة نعرف خطأ عمرو بن عبيد وواصل بن عطاء في التوقف في أمرهم وقولهما: لا ندري من المصيب منهم ومن المخطئ، وخطأ ضرار ومعمر وأبي الهذيل في قولهم: نعلم أن أحدهما مصيب والآخر مخطئ، ونتولى كلا الفريقين على الانفراد لما ثبت بالإجماع عدالتهم فلا تزال بالاختلاف، وهذا مع ما فيه من الفساد للتوقف في أمر علي مع ظهور دلائل إصابته فساد جدًا إذ موالاة أحد الشخصين على الانفراد مع العلم أن أحدهما غير مستحق لذلك باطل، لما فيه من موالاة عدو الله عندهم بيقين. ثم نقول: ينبغي لكم على قياس قولكم أن أمرأتين إحداهما أحب لكم ولا تعرفونها بعينها أن تتزوجوا كل واحدة منهما على الانفراد، وكذا في قبر نبي وقبر كافر لا تعرفون كل واحد بعينه يلزمكم أن تقروا بنبوة كل واحد على الانفراد، وهو كفر، وظهرت به أيضا جهالة بكر بن عبد ربه رئيس البكرية حيث زعم في علي وطلحة والزبير أنهم منافقون مشركون إلا أنهم من أهل الجنة لقول رسول الله عليه السلام في أهل بدر: "لعل الله تعالى اطلع عليهم فقال: اعملوا ما شئتم فقد غفرت لكم" ومن حكم بنفاق هؤلاء الأجلة وشركهم، ثم جعل المشرك مغفورا له
This type of ignorance in his view: its knowledge is seeking discovery and seeking help from one he trusts and striving in vigilance and caution, and with interpretation in his knowledge and opinion, it is not that there remained upon him a right he did not fulfill or that there was from him what prevented him from obtaining the intended, so this became therefore more effective in excuse for him and greater in the meaning of ignorance because with him is some meaning of the forgetful concerning what is upon him of truth in other than him, and God knows best. And it has been narrated that Aisha - may God be pleased with her and her father - did not fight Ali nor did Ali fight her, but Aisha intended reconciliation between the two groups, then war occurred between them, then Ali honored Aisha and returned her to Medina honored and protected. And Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, one of the mutakallimun of the people of hadith, narrated from some eminent people of knowledge: that the incident between them was not with determination for war but was sudden and by way of each group defending themselves, because each thought the other group had betrayed him because the matter had been arranged between them, peace was concluded and they dispersed with satisfaction. Then the killers of Uthman feared being caught and surrounded, so they gathered and consulted and differed, then their opinions agreed that they should become two groups and begin war in the two camps, and mix together, and the group in Ali's camp would shout 'Talha and Zubayr have betrayed,' and the second group would shout 'Ali has betrayed.' So they accomplished that, and war broke out, and each group was defending themselves, and this is correctness from both groups. Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said: This is the correct view, and by this opinion blame is removed from both groups. Then however the story goes, we know that Ali, Talha, and Zubayr were among the ten who were given glad tidings of Paradise, and likewise Aisha was upon her high rank. So whoever extends his tongue in slandering them is the one slandered in his religion, judged to be in error and innovation - may God protect us from that. And by understanding this summary we know the error of Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata in suspending judgment concerning them and their saying: 'We do not know who among them is correct and who is mistaken,' and the error of Dirar, Muammar, and Abu al-Hudhayl in their saying: 'We know that one of them is correct and the other is mistaken.' And we affirm loyalty to each group individually because their uprightness (adala) is established by consensus and is not removed by disagreement. And this, along with its corruption, is the corruption of suspending judgment concerning Ali when evidence of his correctness is evident. For showing loyalty to one of the two persons individually while knowing that one of them does not deserve that is false, because it involves loyalty to an enemy of God, in their view, with certainty. Then we say: It should be for you, by analogy with your statement, that if there are two women, one of whom is beloved to you and you do not know her specifically, you would marry each one individually. Likewise, with a grave of a prophet and a grave of an unbeliever, if you do not know each one specifically, it would be incumbent on you to affirm prophethood of each one individually, which is disbelief. And by this also appears the ignorance of Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih, the head of the Bakriyya, where he claimed concerning Ali, Talha, and Zubayr that they are hypocrites and polytheists, except that they are from the people of Paradise because of the saying of the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, concerning the people of Badr: 'Perhaps God Almighty looked upon them and said: Do whatever you wish, for I have forgiven you.' And he who judges these eminent ones as hypocrites and polytheists, then makes the polytheist forgiven...
Source pages p. 1176

Commentary
This passage is a theological defense of the Companions involved in the Battle of the Camel (al-Jamal) and a refutation of various sectarian opinions. It first discusses a type of ignorance that is excusable when accompanied by seeking knowledge and caution. Then it narrates the historical account: Aisha (رضي الله عنها) did not intend war, and the battle was accidental, provoked by the killers of Uthman who feared justice. Abu Bakr al-Baqillani reports that both groups were defending themselves, thus both were correct. The author affirms that Ali, Talha, Zubayr, and Aisha are among the righteous, and anyone who slanders them is himself condemned. The passage then refutes the positions of Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata, who suspended judgment, and of Dirar, Muammar, and Abu al-Hudhayl, who claimed one side was right and the other wrong. The correct view, the author argues, is to love both groups because their justice (adala) is established by consensus. He further refutes Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih, who inconsistently called them hypocrites yet believed they are from Paradise. The author uses a reductio ad absurdum argument: if one must choose without knowledge, absurdities like marrying two unknown women or affirming prophethood of an unknown grave would follow. Thus, the only sound position is to affirm the uprightness of all parties involved.

Key Terms
جهل
تأويل
عائشة
أبو بكر الباقلاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Aisha and Ali fought each other intentionally, implying sin.
Correction: The passage shows Aisha sought reconciliation, and the battle was provoked by the killers of Uthman, not by the Companions themselves.

Checkpoint
According to the account of Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, why did the war break out between the two groups?
A) Because Aisha insisted on fighting Ali.
B) Because Ali attacked first.
C) Because the killers of Uthman spread false rumors and provoked both sides.
D) Because Talha and Zubayr betrayed Ali.

Key Takeaway
The Battle of the Camel was a tragic misunderstanding; the involved Companions were not sinful but acted in self-defense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about conflicts among Muslims, especially early ones, what is your immediate judgment? Can you hold back from condemning either party until you have full knowledge? What steps will you take to seek the truth before forming an opinion?
?
The Companions in this event were caught in a situation beyond their control, yet they are excused. When you find yourself in a misunderstanding with a fellow Muslim, do you assume their intention is bad, or do you give them the benefit of the doubt as the Companions did? How can you emulate their caution and vigilance?
?
The killers of Uthman exploited the situation to cause division. Are there people today who exploit disagreements among Muslims to widen the rift? How can you avoid being a tool for such division, and what practical steps can you take to promote reconciliation?

Refuting Wrongful Views on the Camel Dispute
Refutes suspension of judgment and forced choice on the Battle of the Camel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the positions of suspension of judgment and taking sides regarding the Battle of the Camel.

Core Idea
You cannot suspend judgment or claim one side was right; both parties were upright.

After establishing the historical narrative, the author refutes three erroneous positions: suspending judgment (Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata), claiming one side is correct and the other wrong (Dirar, Muammar, Abu al-Hudhayl), and outright condemnation (Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih). This lesson focuses on the first two refutations and the declaration that all involved are righteous.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of suspension
Identify the position of Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata who said 'We do not know who is correct.' Understand why the author calls this error.
The author argues that their uprightness (adala) is established by consensus, so we must affirm both.
2
Read the refutation of taking sides
Notice the position of Dirar, Muammar, and Abu al-Hudhayl who said 'We know one is correct and one is wrong.' The author rejects this because we cannot know with certainty.
The author says this leads to a 'certainty of loyalty to an enemy of God' which is false.
3
Understand the correct stance
The author asserts that we must show loyalty to both groups individually because their justice is established by consensus and not removed by disagreement.
This is the Sunni position: respect all Companions involved.
4
Reflect on the ruling
Consider the warning: whoever slanders these Companions is himself condemned in religion.
This underscores the gravity of defaming the Sahaba.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا النوع من الجهل عند نفسه علمه طلب الكشف والاستعانة بمن يثق به والاجتهاد في التيقظ والتحفظ، وليس مع التأويل في علمه ورأيه أنه قد بقي عليه حق لم يَفِ به أو كان منه ما منعه عن حصول المقصود، فصار هذا لذلك أبلغ في العذر له وأعظم في معنى الجهالة لما معه بعض معاني الناسي عما يكون عليه من الحق في غيره، والله أعلم.
وقد روي أن عائشة - رضي الله عنها وعن أبيها - لم تحارب عليا ولا حاربها علي، وإنما قصدت عائشة الإصلاح بين الطائفيتن فوقع الحرب بينهما، ثم أكرم علي عائشة وردها إلى المدينة مكرمة مصونة.
وروى أبو بكر الباقلاني أحد متكلمي أهل الحديث عن بعض الأجلة من أهل العلم: أن الواقعة بينهم كانت على غير عزيمة على الحرب بل كانت فجأةً وعلى سبيل دفع كل واحد من الفريقين عن أنفسهم، لظنه أن الفريق الآخر قد غدر به لأن الأمر كان قد انتظم بينهم وتم الصلح والتفرق على الرضا، فخاف قتلة عثمان من التمكن منهم والإحاطة بهم، فاجتمعوا وتشاوروا واختلفوا ثم اتفقت آراؤهم على أن يصيروا فريقين ويبدأوا بالحرب في المعسكرين، ويختلطوا ويصيح الفريق الذي في عسكر علي له به غدر طلحة والزبير، ويصيح الفريق الثاني: غدر علي، فتم لهم ذلك، ونشبت الحرب، فكان كل فريق منهم دافعا عن نفسه، وهذا صواب من الفريقين، قال أبو بكر الباقلاني : هذا هو الصحيح، وعلى هذا الرأي اندفعت اللائمة عن الفريقين، ثم كيفما دارت القصة فنحن نعلم أن عليًا وطلحة والزبير كانوا من العشرة الذين بشروا بالجنة، وكذا عائشة كانت على ما كانت عليه من الدرجة الرفيعة، فمن بسط لسانه فيهم بالطعن فهو المطعون في دينه المحكوم عليه بالضلال والبدعة - عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك، وبالإحاطة بهذه الجملة نعرف خطأ عمرو بن عبيد وواصل بن عطاء في التوقف في أمرهم وقولهما: لا ندري من المصيب منهم ومن المخطئ، وخطأ ضرار ومعمر وأبي الهذيل في قولهم: نعلم أن أحدهما مصيب والآخر مخطئ، ونتولى كلا الفريقين على الانفراد لما ثبت بالإجماع عدالتهم فلا تزال بالاختلاف، وهذا مع ما فيه من الفساد للتوقف في أمر علي مع ظهور دلائل إصابته فساد جدًا إذ موالاة أحد الشخصين على الانفراد مع العلم أن أحدهما غير مستحق لذلك باطل، لما فيه من موالاة عدو الله عندهم بيقين. ثم نقول: ينبغي لكم على قياس قولكم أن أمرأتين إحداهما أحب لكم ولا تعرفونها بعينها أن تتزوجوا كل واحدة منهما على الانفراد، وكذا في قبر نبي وقبر كافر لا تعرفون كل واحد بعينه يلزمكم أن تقروا بنبوة كل واحد على الانفراد، وهو كفر، وظهرت به أيضا جهالة بكر بن عبد ربه رئيس البكرية حيث زعم في علي وطلحة والزبير أنهم منافقون مشركون إلا أنهم من أهل الجنة لقول رسول الله عليه السلام في أهل بدر: "لعل الله تعالى اطلع عليهم فقال: اعملوا ما شئتم فقد غفرت لكم" ومن حكم بنفاق هؤلاء الأجلة وشركهم، ثم جعل المشرك مغفورا له
This type of ignorance in his view: its knowledge is seeking discovery and seeking help from one he trusts and striving in vigilance and caution, and with interpretation in his knowledge and opinion, it is not that there remained upon him a right he did not fulfill or that there was from him what prevented him from obtaining the intended, so this became therefore more effective in excuse for him and greater in the meaning of ignorance because with him is some meaning of the forgetful concerning what is upon him of truth in other than him, and God knows best. And it has been narrated that Aisha - may God be pleased with her and her father - did not fight Ali nor did Ali fight her, but Aisha intended reconciliation between the two groups, then war occurred between them, then Ali honored Aisha and returned her to Medina honored and protected. And Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, one of the mutakallimun of the people of hadith, narrated from some eminent people of knowledge: that the incident between them was not with determination for war but was sudden and by way of each group defending themselves, because each thought the other group had betrayed him because the matter had been arranged between them, peace was concluded and they dispersed with satisfaction. Then the killers of Uthman feared being caught and surrounded, so they gathered and consulted and differed, then their opinions agreed that they should become two groups and begin war in the two camps, and mix together, and the group in Ali's camp would shout 'Talha and Zubayr have betrayed,' and the second group would shout 'Ali has betrayed.' So they accomplished that, and war broke out, and each group was defending themselves, and this is correctness from both groups. Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said: This is the correct view, and by this opinion blame is removed from both groups. Then however the story goes, we know that Ali, Talha, and Zubayr were among the ten who were given glad tidings of Paradise, and likewise Aisha was upon her high rank. So whoever extends his tongue in slandering them is the one slandered in his religion, judged to be in error and innovation - may God protect us from that. And by understanding this summary we know the error of Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata in suspending judgment concerning them and their saying: 'We do not know who among them is correct and who is mistaken,' and the error of Dirar, Muammar, and Abu al-Hudhayl in their saying: 'We know that one of them is correct and the other is mistaken.' And we affirm loyalty to each group individually because their uprightness (adala) is established by consensus and is not removed by disagreement. And this, along with its corruption, is the corruption of suspending judgment concerning Ali when evidence of his correctness is evident. For showing loyalty to one of the two persons individually while knowing that one of them does not deserve that is false, because it involves loyalty to an enemy of God, in their view, with certainty. Then we say: It should be for you, by analogy with your statement, that if there are two women, one of whom is beloved to you and you do not know her specifically, you would marry each one individually. Likewise, with a grave of a prophet and a grave of an unbeliever, if you do not know each one specifically, it would be incumbent on you to affirm prophethood of each one individually, which is disbelief. And by this also appears the ignorance of Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih, the head of the Bakriyya, where he claimed concerning Ali, Talha, and Zubayr that they are hypocrites and polytheists, except that they are from the people of Paradise because of the saying of the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, concerning the people of Badr: 'Perhaps God Almighty looked upon them and said: Do whatever you wish, for I have forgiven you.' And he who judges these eminent ones as hypocrites and polytheists, then makes the polytheist forgiven...
Source pages p. 1176

Commentary
This passage is a theological defense of the Companions involved in the Battle of the Camel (al-Jamal) and a refutation of various sectarian opinions. It first discusses a type of ignorance that is excusable when accompanied by seeking knowledge and caution. Then it narrates the historical account: Aisha (رضي الله عنها) did not intend war, and the battle was accidental, provoked by the killers of Uthman who feared justice. Abu Bakr al-Baqillani reports that both groups were defending themselves, thus both were correct. The author affirms that Ali, Talha, Zubayr, and Aisha are among the righteous, and anyone who slanders them is himself condemned. The passage then refutes the positions of Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata, who suspended judgment, and of Dirar, Muammar, and Abu al-Hudhayl, who claimed one side was right and the other wrong. The correct view, the author argues, is to love both groups because their justice (adala) is established by consensus. He further refutes Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih, who inconsistently called them hypocrites yet believed they are from Paradise. The author uses a reductio ad absurdum argument: if one must choose without knowledge, absurdities like marrying two unknown women or affirming prophethood of an unknown grave would follow. Thus, the only sound position is to affirm the uprightness of all parties involved.

Key Terms
عمرو بن عبيد
واصل بن عطاء
ضرار
معمر
أبو الهذيل
عدالة الصحابة
العشرة المبشرون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is permissible to suspend judgment about the Companions in the Battle of the Camel (as the Mu'tazila did).
Correction: The author argues that since their uprightness is consensually established, we must affirm both parties.

Checkpoint
What was the position of Amr ibn Ubayd and Wasil ibn Ata regarding the parties in the Battle of the Camel?
A) They said both sides were correct.
B) They said one side was correct and the other mistaken.
C) They suspended judgment and said 'We do not know who is correct and who is mistaken.'
D) They said both sides were mistaken.

Key Takeaway
Suspending judgment or taking sides regarding the Battle of the Camel is erroneous; we must uphold the justice of all involved Companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about disagreements among Muslims, do you feel compelled to take a side? How does this lesson's insistence on upholding the 'adala of both parties challenge your instinct? Will you try to assume the best in all involved before forming an opinion?
?
Think of a recent conflict in your community. Did you privately think someone was entirely at fault? If the principle of 'adala applies to the Companions despite their dispute, how might you extend that same charity to others? What concrete step can you take today to avoid slandering anyone in a disagreement?
?
If you were present during the Battle of the Camel, which side would your emotions have led you to support? Now, knowing that both were upright, how does that realization affect your understanding of loyalty in conflicts? Are you willing to love all parties equally, even when your heart wants to pick one?

Sects and Disagreements
advanced
12 min
The Absurdity of the Bakriyya Position
Refutes Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih using logical analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih's claim that the Companions were hypocrites yet forgiven is inconsistent and how the author uses reductio ad absurdum to refute it.

Core Idea
You cannot call them hypocrites yet claim they are forgiven.

The final part of the source refutes the extremist Bakriyya sect. Bakr ibn Abd Rabbih claimed that Ali, Talha, and Zubayr were hypocrites and polytheists but still among the people of Paradise due to the hadith about Badr. The author shows this is self-contradictory and uses a powerful logical analogy: if you cannot distinguish between two women, you cannot marry both; similarly, you cannot affirm prophethood of an unknown grave. Thus, the only consistent position is to affirm the righteousness of all Companions without exception.

Guided Path
1
Read Bakr's claim
Understand the contradictory position: they are hypocrites and polytheists yet forgiven and from Paradise.
The author calls this 'جهالة' (ignorance).
2
Analyze the reductio argument
The author gives two analogies: marrying two women when only one is beloved, and affirming prophethood of an unknown grave. These lead to absurdities.
The point: if you cannot know, you cannot treat both equally; you must have a way to distinguish.
3
Understand the conclusion
The only sound position is to affirm the uprightness of all Companions involved and avoid contradictory claims.

هذا النوع من الجهل عند نفسه علمه طلب الكشف والاستعانة بمن يثق به والاجتهاد في التيقظ والتحفظ، وليس مع التأويل في علمه ورأيه أنه قد بقي عليه حق لم يَفِ به أو كان منه ما منعه عن حصول المقصود، فصار هذا لذلك أبلغ في العذر له وأعظم في معنى الجهالة لما معه بعض معاني الناسي عما يكون عليه من الحق في غيره، والله أعلم.
وقد روي أن عائشة - رضي الله عنها وعن أبيها - لم تحارب عليا ولا حاربها علي، وإنما قصدت عائشة الإصلاح بين الطائفيتن فوقع الحرب بينهما، ثم أكرم علي عائشة وردها إلى المدينة مكرمة مصونة.

It first discusses a type of ignorance that is excusable when accompanied by seeking knowledge and caution. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can consider the Companions as hypocrites yet still believe they are forgiven and from Paradise.
